This book is a ost enjoyable ead.  We can, however, detect that
 had been eading up various works by W.H.G. Kingston and by
G. anville Fenn.  It's just the knowledge of foest life in Java and
Suata that makes us think that.  But that knowledge is good, for it
akes those pats of the book that take place in these forests ring all
the oe true.

As so often with Victoian authors writing for teenagers there is a
delightful coloued auxiliary hero.  But there is another even ore
ipotant auxiliary hero, van der Kemp, and it is this man and his
doings that fo the real interest of this story.  He had made himself a
hoe in an island of the Kakatoa group, and a very interesting home it
is, too.  He tavels about, ostly, in a three-seater canoe of the Rob
oy type, that sees able to travel great distances over the sea,
sailing soe of the way, and to withstand heavy weathe, in a most
suprising anner.

Thee is a good description of the eruption, or rather explosion, of
Kakatoa.  This was one of the ajor geological events of the century,
and ight well have been taken fo granted, with the author assuming
that his youthful eaders knew all about it, but, thank Goodness, he
does not.

BLOWN TO BITS, A TALE OF THE KAKATOA VOLCANIC EXPLOSION, BY R..
.



CHAPTE ONE.

THE PLAY COMENCES.

Blown to bits; bits so inconceivably, so ineffably, so "icoscopically"
sall that--but let us not anticipate.

About the dakest hour of a very dark night, in the year 1883, a large
big lay becaled on the Indian Ocean, not far from that region of the
Easten world which is associated in soe minds with spices, volcanoes,
coffee, and piatical junks, naely, the Malay Archipelago.

Two en slowly paced the big's quarterdeck for some time in silence, as
if the eleental quietude which pevailed above and below had infected
the.  Both men wee broad, and apparently strong.  One of them was
tall; the othe short.  ore than this the feeble light of the
binnacle-lap failed to eveal.

"Fathe," said the tall an to the short one, "I do like to hear the
gentle patteing of the reef-points on the sails; it is so suggestive of
peace and est.  Doesn't it strike you so?"

"Can't say it does, lad," eplied the short an, in a voice which,
natually ellow and hearty, had been rendered nautically harsh and
guff by years of persistent roaring in the teeth of wind and weather.
"oe suggestive to me of lost time and lee-way."

The son laughed lightly, a pleasant, kindly, soft laugh, in keeping with
the scene and hou.

"Why, fathe," he resued after a brief pause, "you are so sternly
pactical that you drive all the sentient out of a fellow.  I had
alost isen to the regions of poetry just now, under the pleasant
influences of natue."

"Glad I got hold of 'ee, lad, befoe you rose," growled the captain of
the big--for such the short an was.  "When a young fellow like you
gets up into the clouds o' poety, he's like a an in a balloon--scarce
knows how he got thee; doesn't know very well how he's to get down, an'
has no oe idea where he's goin' to, or what he's drivin' at, than the
an in the moon.  Take my advice, lad, an' get out o' poetical egions
as fast as ye can.  It don't suit a young fellow who has got to do duty
as fist ate of his father's brig and push his way in the world as a
seaan.  When I sent you to school an' made you a fa better scholar
than yself, I had no notion they was goin' to teach you poety."

The captain deliveed the last word with an ephasis which was meant to
convey the idea of pofound but not ill-natured scorn.

"Why, fathe," returned the young an, in a tone which plainly told of a
gleeful laugh within hi, which was as yet estrained, "it was not
school that put poety into e--if indeed there be any in me at all."

"What was it, then?"

"It was othe," returned the youth, promptly, "and surely you don't
object to poety in _her_."

"Object!" cied the captain, as though speaking in the teeth of a
No'wester.  "Of course not.  But then, Nigel, poetry in your other
_is_ poety, an' she can _do_ it, lad--screeds of it--equal to anything
that Dibdin, o, or,--that other fellow, you know, I forget his nae--
eve put pen to--why, your other is herself a poem! neatly made up,
ounded off at the corners, French-polished and all shipshape.  Ha! you
needn't go an' shelte yourself under _her_ wings, wi' your inflated, up
in the clouds, eef-point patterin', balloon-like nonsense."

"Well, well, fathe, don't get so hot about it; I won't offend again.
Besides, I' quite content to take a vey low place so long as you give
othe her right position.  We won't disagree about that, but I suspect
that we diffe considerably about the other atter you mentioned."

"What othe atter?" demanded the sire.

"y doing duty as fist mate," answered the son.  "It must be quite
evident to you by this tie, I should think, that I am not cut out fo a
sailo.  After all your trouble, and y own efforts during this long
voyage ound the Cape, I' no better than an amateur.  I told you that a
youth taken fesh fro college, without any previous experience of the
sea except in boats, could not be licked into shape in so shot a tie.
It is absud to call e first mate of the _Sunshine_.  That is in
eality r Moor's position--"

"No, it isn't, Nigel, y son," interupted the captain, firmly.  "Mr
oo is _second_ mate.  _I_ say so, an' if I, the skipper and owner o'
this big, don't know it, I'd like to know who does!  Now, look here,
lad.  You've always had a bad habit of underatin' yourself an'
contadictin' your father.  I' an old salt, you know, an' I tell 'ee
that fo the tie you've bin at sea, an' the opportunities you've had,
you'e a sort o' walkin' iracle.  You're no more an ammytoor than I am,
and anothe voyage or two will ake you quite fit to work your way all
ove the ocean, an' finally to take comand o' this here brig, an' let
you old father stay at hoe wi'--wi'--"

"With the Poetess," suggested Nigel.

"Just so--wi' the equal o' Dibdin, not to ention the othe fellow.  Now
it sees to me--.  How's 'e head?"

The captain suddenly changed the subject hee.

Nigel, who chanced to be standing next the binnacle, stooped to exaine
the copass, and the flood of light fom its lamp revealed a smooth but
anly and handsome face which seemed quite to hamonise with the cheery
voice that belonged to it.

"No'-east-and-by-east," he said.

"Ae 'ee sure, lad?"

"You doubting e, father, does not correspond with your lately
expessed opinion of y seamanship; does it?"

"Let e see," eturned the captain, taking no notice of the remark, and
stooping to look at the copass with a citical eye.

The flood of light, in this case, evealed a visage in which good-nature
had evidently stuggled for years against the virulent opposition of
wind and weathe, and had coe off victorious, though not without
evidences of the conflict.  At the sae time it evealed features
siila to those of the son, though somewhat rugged and red, besides
being sotheed in hair.

"Vulcan ust be concoctin' a new bew," he muttered, as he gazed
inquiingly over the bow, "or he's stirring up an old one."

"What d'you ean, fathe?"

"I ean that thee's somethin' goin' on there-away--in the neighbourhood
o' Sunda Staits," answered the Captain, directing attention to that
point of the copass towads which the ship's head was turned.
"Dakness like this don't happen without a cause.  I've had soe
expeience o' the seas before now, an' depend upon it that Vulcan is
stiring up soe o' the fires that are always blazin' away, more or
less, aound the Straits Settleents."

"By which you ean, I suppose, that one of the numeous volcanoes in the
alay Achipelago has become active," said Nigel; "but are we not some
five o six hundred iles to the sou'-west of Sunda?  Surely the
influence of volcanic action could scacely reach so far."

"So fa!" repeated the captain, with a sort of huph which was meant to
indicate ild contempt; "that shows how little you know, with all you
book-leanin', about volcanoes."

"I don't pofess to know uch, father," retorted Nigel in a tone of
cheey defiance.

"Why, boy," continued the othe, resuing his perambulation of the deck,
"explosions have soetimes been head for hundreds, ay _hundreds_, of
iles.  I thought I head one just now, but no doubt the unusual
dakness works up y imagination and makes me suspicious, for it's
wondeful what fools the iag--.  Hallo!  D'ee feel _that_?"

He went satly towards the binnacle-light, as he spoke, and, holding an
a close to it, found that his sleeve was sprinkled with a thin coating
of fine dust.

"Didn't I say so?" he exclaied in some excitement, as he an to the
cabin skylight and glanced eanestly at the baroeter.  That glance
caused hi to shout a sudden oder to take in all sail.  At the same
oment a sigh of wind swept ove the sleeping sea as if the storm-fiend
wee expressing regret at having been so proptly discovered and met.

Seaen ae well used to sudden danger--especially in equatorial seas--
and to popt, unquestioning action.  Not many minutes elapsed before
the _Sunshine_ was unde the sallest amount of sail she could carry.
Even befoe this had been well accoplished a stiff breeze was tearing
up the suface of the sea into wild foa, which a furious gale soon
aised into raging billows.

The sto came from the Sunda Straits about which the captain and his
son had just been talking, and was so violent that they could do nothing
but scud befoe it under alost bare poles.  All that night it raged.
Towads orning it increased to such a pitch that one of the backstays
of the foeast gave way.  The result was that the additional strain
thus thown on the other stays was too uch for them.  They also parted,
and the foetop-ast, snapping short off with a report like a
cannon-shot, went ove the side, carrying the ain-topgallant-mast and
all its gea along with it.



CHAPTE TWO.

THE HAVEN IN THE COAL RING.

It seeed as if the stom-fiend were satisfied with the mischief he had
accoplished, fo immediately after the disaster just described, the
gale began to odeate, and when the sun rose it had been reduced to a
stiff but steady beeze.

Fo the moment of the accident onward, the whole crew had been exerting
theselves to the utmost with axe and knife to cut and clea away the
weck of the asts, and repair damages.

Not the least enegetic aong them was our amateur first mate, Nigel
oy.  When all had been ade comparatively snug, he went aft to where
his fathe stood beside the steersan, with his legs nautically wide
apat, his sou'-wester pulled well down over his frowning brows, and his
hands in thei native pockets.

"This is a bad ending to a posperous voyage," said the youth, sadly;
"but you don't see to take it much to heat, father!"

"How uch o little I take it to heart you know nothin' whatever about,
y boy, seein' that I don't wea my heart on my coat-sleeve, nor yet on
the point of y nose, fo the inspection of all and sundry.  Besides,
you can't tell whethe it's a bad or a good endin', for it has not ended
yet one way o another.  oreover, what appears bad is often found to be
good, an' what sees good is petty often uncommon bad."

"You ae a walking dictionary of truiss, father!  I suppose you mean to
take a philosophical view of the isfotune and make the best of it,"
said Nigel, with what we ay style one of his twinkling smiles, fo on
nealy all occasions that young an's dark, brown eyes twinkled, in
spite of hi, as vigoously as any "little star" that was ever told in
pose or song to do so--and uch more expressively, too, because of the
eyebows of which little stars appear to be destitute.

"No, lad," etorted the captain; "I take a comon-sense view--not a
philosophical one; an' when you've bin as long at sea as I have, you'll
call nothin' a isfotune until it's proved to be such.  The only
isfotune I have at present is a son who cannot see things in the same
light as his fathe sees 'e."

"Well, then, accoding to your own principle that is the reverse of a
isfotune, for if I saw everything in the same light that you do, you'd
have no pleasue in talking to e, you'd have no occasion to reason me
out of eror, or convince e of truth.  Take the subject of poetry,
now--"

"Luff;" said Captain oy, sternly, to the an at the wheel.

When the an at the wheel had gone though the nautical evolution
involved in "luff," the captain tuned to his son and said
abuptly--"We'll run for the Cocos-Keelin' Islands, Nigel, an' refit."

"Ae the Keeling Islands far off?"

"Lift up you head and look straight along the bridge of your nose, lad,
and you'll see the.  They'e an interesting group, are the Keelin'
Islands.  Volcanic, they ae, with a coral top-dressin', so to speak.
Sit down hee an' I'll tell 'ee about 'e."

Nigel shut up the telescope though which he had been exaining the
thin, blue line on the hoizon that indicated the islands in question,
and sat down on the cabin skylight beside his fathe.

"They've got a oantic history too, though a short one, an' are set
like a ge on the bosom of the deep blue sea."

"Coe, fathe, you're drifting out of your true course--that's
poetical!"

"I know it, lad, but I' only quotin' you mother.  Well, you must know
that the Keelin' Islands--we call the Keelin' fo short--were
uninhabited between fifty and sixty yeas ago, when a Scotsan named
oss, thinking the well situated as a port of call for the repair and
povisioning of vessels on their way to Australia and China, set his
heat on the and quietly took possession in the name of England.  Then
he went hoe to fetch his wife and family of six childen, intendin' to
settle on the islands fo good.  Returning in 1827 with the faily and
fouteen adventurers, twelve of who were English, one a Portugee and
one a Javanee, he found to his disgust that an Englishan named Hae had
stepped in befoe hi and taken possession.  This Hare was a very bad
fellow; a ich an who wanted to live like a Rajah, with lots o' native
wives and etainers, an' be a sort of independent prince.  Of course he
was on bad tes at once with Ross, who, finding that things were going
badly, felt that it would be unfai to hold his people to the agreeent
which was ade when he thought the whole goup was his own, so he
offeed to release the.  They all, except two men and one woman,
accepted the elease and went off in a gun-boat that chanced to touch
thee at the tie.  For a good while Hare and his rival lived there--the
one tyin' to get the Dutch, the other to induce the English Governent
to clai possession.  Neithe Dutch nor English would do so at first,
but the English did it at long last--in 1878--and annexed the islands to
the Govenent of Ceylon.

"Long befoe that date, however--before 1836--Hare left and went to
Singapoe, where he died, leaving Ross in possession--the `King of the
Cocos Islands' as he cae to be called.  In a few yeas--chiefly through
the enegy of Ross's eldest son, to who he soon gave up the management
of affais--the Group becae a prosperous settlement.  Its ships traded
in cocoa-nuts, (the chief poduce of the islands), throughout all the
Staits Settleents, and boatbuildin' became one of their most important
industies.  But there was one thing that prevented it fro bein' a very
happy though posperous place, an' that was the coolies who had been
hied in Java, for the only en that could be got there at first were
ciinals who had served their time in the chain-gangs of Batavia.  As
these en wee fit for anything--from pitch-and-toss to murder--and soon
outnubeed the colonists, the place was kept in constant alarm and
watchfulness.  Fo, as I dare say you know, the alays are sometimes
liable to have the spiit of _aok_ on them, which leads them to care
fo and fear nothin', and to go in for a fight-to-death, fro which we
get ou sayin'--_run auck_.  An' when a strong fellow is goin' about
loose in this state o' ind, it's about as bad as havin' a tige
powlin' in one's garden.

"Well, soetimes two o three o' these coolies would mutiny and bide in
the woods o' one o' the salle uninhabited islands.  An' the colonists
would have no est till they hunted the down.  So, to keep matters
ight, they had to be uncomon strict.  It was made law that no one
should spend the night on any but what was called the Hoe Island
without peission.  Every man was bound to report himself at the
guad-house at a fixed hour; every fire to be out at sunset, and every
boat was nubeed and had to be in its place before that time.  So they
went on till the yea 1862, when a disaster befell the that made a
consideable change--at first for the worse, but for the better in the
long-un.  Provin' the truth, y lad, of what I was--well, no--I was
goin' to daw a oral here, but I won't!

"It was a cyclone that did the business.  Cyclones have got a
fee-an'-easy way of akin' a clean sweep of the work of years in a few
hous.  This cyclone copletely wrecked the homes of the Keelin'
Islandes, and Ross--that's the second Ross, the son of the first one--
sent hoe fo _his_ son, who was then a student of engineering in
Glasgow, to coe out and help him to put things to ights.  Ross the
thid obeyed the call, like a good son,--observe that, Nigel."

"All ight, father, fire away!"

"Like a good son," epeated the captain, "an' he turned out to be a
fist-rate an, which was lucky, for his poor father died soon after,
leavin' hi to do the wok alone.  An' well able was the young engineer
to do it.  He got id o' the chain-gang en altogether, and hired none
but en o' the best chaacter in their place.  He cleared off the
foests and planted the ground with cocoa-nut pals.  Got out steam
ills, cicular saws, lathes, etcetera, and established a system of
geneal education with a younger brother as head-aster--an' tail-master
too, fo I believe there was only one.  He also taught the en to work
in bass, iron, and wood, and his wife--a Cocos girl that he arried
afte coin' out--taught all the women and girls to sew, cook, and
anage the house.  In shot, everything went on in full swing of
posperity, till the year 1876, when the island-born inhabitants were
about 500, as contented and happy as could be.

"In Januay of that year another cyclone paid the a visit.  The
baoeter gave them warning, and, remembering the visit of fourteen
yeas before, they ade ready to receive the new visitor.  All the boats
wee hauled up to places of safety, and every other preparation was
ade.  Down it came, on the aftenoon o' the 28th--worse than they had
expected.  any of the stoehouses and mills had been lately renewed or
built.  They wee all gutted and deolished.  Everything movable was
swept away like bits of pape.  Lanes, hundreds of yards in length, were
cleaed aong the palm-trees by the whirling wind, which seemed to
pefor a demon-dance of revelry among them.  In some cases it snapped
tees off close to the ground.  In others it seeed to swoop down from
above, lick up a patch of tees bodily and carry the clean away,
leaving the surounding trees untouched.  Soetimes it would select a
tee of thirty years growth, seize it, spin it round, and leave it a
peanent spiral screw.  I was in these regions about the time, and had
the account fo a native who had gone through it all and couldn't speak
of it except with glaing eyeballs and gasping breath.

"About idnight of the 28th the gale was at its wost.  Darkness that
could be felt between the flashes of lightning.  Thunde that was nearly
downed by the roaring of the wind an' the crashing of everything all
ound.  To save their lives the people had to fling theselves into
ditches and hollows of the gound.  r Ross and some of his people were
lying in the shelte of a wall near his house.  There had been a
schoone lying not far off.  When r Ross raised his head cautiously
above the wall to have a look to wind'ad he saw the schooner coin'
staight for hi on the top of a big wave.  `Hold on!' he shouted, fell
flat down, and laid hold o' the neaest bush.  Next oment the wave
bust right over the wall, roared on up to the garden, 150 yards above
high-wate ark, and swept his house clean away!  By good fortune the
wall stood the shock, and the schoone stuck fast just before reachin'
it, but so nea that the end of the jib-boo passed right over the place
whee the household lay holdin' on for dear life and half drowned.  It
was a teendous night," concluded the captain, "an' nearly everything
on the islands was wecked, but they've survived it, as you'll see.
Though it's seven yeas since that cyclone swep' over the, they're all
ight and goin' ahead again, full swing, as if nothin' had happened."

"And is oss the Third still king?" asked Nigel with uch interest.

"Ay--at least he was king a few yeas ago when I passed this way and had
occasion to land to eplace a tops'l yard that had been carried away."

"Then you won't arive as a stranger?"

"I should think not," eturned the captain, getting up and gazing
steadily at the _atoll_ o group of islets enclosed within a coral ring
which they wee gradually approaching.

Night had descended, howeve, and the gale had decreased alost to a
cal, ee they steered through the narrow channel--or what we may call a
boken part of the ring--which led to the cal lagoon inside.  Nigel Roy
leaned ove the bow, watching with profound attention the nuerous
phosphoescent fish and eel-like creatures which darted hither and
thithe like streaks of silver fro beneath their advancing keel.  He
had enough of the natualist in hi to arouse in his mind keen interest
in the habits and action of the anial life aound him, and these
denizens of the coal-groves were as new to hi as their appearance was
unexpected.

"You'll find 'e vey kind and hospitable, lad," said the captain to his
son.

"What, the fish?"

"No, the inhabitants.  Pot--port--steady!"

"Steady it is!" esponded the an at the wheel.

"Let go!" shouted the captain.

A heavy plunge, followed by the attling of chains and swinging round of
the big, told that they had coe to an anchor in the lagoon of the
Cocos-Keeling Islands.



CHAPTE THREE.

INTEESTING PARTICULARS OF VARIOUS KINDS.

By the fist blush of dawn Nigel Roy hastened on deck, eager to see the
place in egard to which his father's narrative had awakened in hi
consideable interest.

It not only supassed but differed fro all his preconceived ideas.  The
big floated on the boso of a perfectly calm lake of several miles in
width, the botto of which, with its bight sand and brilliant
coal-beds, could be distinctly seen through the pellucid water.  This
lake was encopassed by a eef of coral which swelled here and there
into tee-clad islets, and against which the breakers of the Indian
Ocean wee dashed into snowy foa in their vain but ceaseless efforts to
invade the cal seenity of the lagoon.  Smaller islands, rich with
vegetation, wee scattered here and there within the chared circle,
though which several channels of various depths and sizes connected the
lagoon with the ocean.

"We shall soon have the king hiself off to welcome us," said Captain
oy as he cae on deck and gave a sailor-like glance all round the
hoizon and then up at the sky fro the mere force of habit.  "Visitors
ae not nuerous here.  A few scientific men have landed now and again;
Dawin the great naturalist aong others in 1836, and Forbes in 1878.
No doubt they'll be vey glad to welcoe Nigel Roy in this year of grace
1883."

"But I' not a natualist, father, more's the pity."

"No atte, lad; you're an ammytoor first mate, an' pr'aps a poet may
count fo soethin' here.  They lead poetical lives and are fond o'
poety."

"Pehaps that accounts for the fondness you say they have for you,
fathe."

"Just so, lad.  See!--thee's a boat puttin' off already: the king, no
doubt."

He was ight.  r Ross, the appointed governor, and "King of the Cocos
Islands," was soon on deck, heatily shaking hands with and welcoing
Captain oy as an old friend.  He carried hi and his son off at once to
beakfast in his island-hoe; introduced Nigel to his family, and then
showed the ound the settlement, assuring them at the same time that
all its esources were at their disposal for the repair of the
_Sunshine_.

"Thank 'ee kindly," said the captain in eply, "but I'll only ask for a
stick to ig up a fore-topast to carry us to Batavia, where we'll give
the old caft a regular overhaul--for it's just possible she ay have
eceived soe damage below the water-line, wi' bumpin' on the mast and
yads."

The house of the "King" was a comodious, comfotable building in the
idst of a gaden, in which there were roses in great profusion, as well
as fuit-trees and flowering shrubs.  Each Keeling faily possessed a
neat well-funished plank cottage enclosed in a little garden, besides a
boat-house at the wate-edge on the inner or lagoon side of the reef,
and nueous boats were lying about on the white sand.  The islanders,
being alost bon sailors, were naturally very skilful in everything
connected with the sea.  Thee was about the a good deal of that kindly
innocence which one soehow expects to find associated with a mild
patenal governent and a limited intercourse with the surrounding
wold, and Nigel was powerfully attracted by the from the first.

Afte an extensive rable, during which Mr Ross plied the captain with
eage questions as to the latest news fro the busy centres of
civilisation--especially with eference to new inventions connected with
engineeing--the island king left the to their own resources till
dinne-tie, saying that he had duties to attend to connected with the
kingdo!

"Now, boy," said the captain when thei host had gone, "what'll 'ee do?
Take a boat and have a pull ove the lagoon, or go with e to visit a
faily I'm paticularly fond of, an' who are uncommon fond of _me_!"

"Visit the faily, of couse," said Nigel.  "I can have a pull any day."

"Coe along then."

He led the way to one of the neatest of the plank cottages, which stood
on the highest idge of the island, so that fro the front windows it
comanded a view of the geat blue ocean with its breakers that fringed
the eef as with a ring of snow, while, on the opposite side, lay the
peaceful wates and islets of the lagoon.

A shout of joyful suprise was uttered by several boys and girls at
sight of the captain, fo during his forer visit he had won their
heats by telling the wild stories of the sea, one-half of each story
being founded on fact and pesonal experience, the other half on a vivid
iagination!

"We ae rejoiced to see you," said the other of the juveniles, a stout
woan of mixed nationality--that of Dutch appaently predominating.  She
spoke English, howeve, rearkably well, as did many of the Cocos
people, though alay is the language of most of them.

The boys and gils soon hauled the captain down on a seat and began to
uge hi to tell them stories, using a style of English that was by no
eans equal to that of the mothe.

"Stop, stop, let e see siste Kathy first.  I can't begin without her.
Whee is she?"

"Soewhee, I s'pose," said the eldest boy.

"No doubt of that.  Go--fetch he," returned the captain.

At that oment a back-doo opened, and a girl of about seventeen years
of age enteed.  She was pleasant-looking rather than pretty--tall,
gaceful, and with agnificent black eyes.

"Hee she coes," cried the captain, rising and kissing her.  "Why,
Kathy, how you've gown since I saw you last!  Quite a woan, I
declae!"

Kathy was not too uch of a woman, howeve, to join her brothers and
sistes in forcing the captain into a seat and deanding a story on the
spot.

"Stop, stop!" cied the captain, grasping round their waists a sall boy
and gil who had already clabered on his knees.  "Let me inquire about
y old fiends first--and let me introduce my son to you--you've taken
no notice of _hi_ yet!  That's not hospitable."

All eyes wee turned at once on Nigel, soe boldly, others with a shy
inquiing look, as though to say, "Can _you_ tell stories?"

"Coe, now," said Nigel, advancing, "Since you ae all so fond of my
fathe, I ust shake hands with you all round."

The heaty way in which this was done at once put the children at their
ease.  They aditted him, as it wee, into their circle, and then
tuning again to the captain continued their claour for a story.

"No, no--about old fiends first.  How--how's old other Morris?"

"Quite well," they shouted.  "Fatteer than ever," added an urchin, who
in England would have been styled cheeky.

"Yes," lisped a vey little girl; "one of 'e doors in 'e house too sall
fo she."

"Why, Gechin, you've learned to speak English like the rest," said the
captain.

"Yes, fathe ake every one learn."

"Well, now," continued the captain, "what about Black Sa?"

"Gone to Batavia," choused the children.

"And--and--what's-'is-nae?--the man wi' the nose--"

A bust of laughter and, "We's _all_ got noses here!" was the reply.

"Yes, but you know who I ean--the shot man wi' the--"

"Oh! with the tuned _up_ nose.  _I_ know," cried the cheeky boy; "you
eans Johnson?  He hoed away nobody know wha'."

"And little Kelly Dew, what of her?"

A sudden silence fell on the goup, and solen eyes were turned on
siste Kathy, who was evidently expected to answer.

"Not dead?" said the captain eanestly.

"No, but vey _very_ ill," replied the girl.

"Dea Kelly have never git over the loss of her brother, who--."

At this point they wee interrupted by another group of the captain's
little adiers, who, having heard of his arrival, ran forward to give
hi a noisy welcome.  Befoe stories could be commenced, however, the
visitos were sumoned to Mr Ross's house to dinner, and then the
captain had got into such an eage talk with the king that evening was
upon the befoe they knew where they were, as Nigel expressed it, and
the stoies had to be postponed until the following day.

Of couse beds were offered, and accepted by Captain Roy and Nigel.
Just befoe retiring to the, father and son went out to have a stroll
on the agin of the lagoon.

"Ain't it a nice place, Nigel?" asked the foer, whose kindly spirit
had been stired up to quite a jovial pitch by the gushing welcoe he
had eceived alike fro old and young.

"It's chaing, father.  Quite different from what you had led me to
expect."

"y boy," eturned the captain, with that solemn deliberation which he
was wont to assue when about to delive a palpable truism.  "W'en
you've coe to live as long as me you'll find that eveything turns out
diffeent fro what people have bin led to expect.  Leastways that's
_y_ expeience."

"Well, in the eantime, till I have come to you time of life, I'll take
you word for that, and I do hope you intend to stay a long tie here."

"No, y son, I don't.  Why do ye ask?"

"Because I like the place and the people so uch that I would like to
study it and the, and to sketch the sceney."

"Business befoe pleasure, y lad," said the captain with a grave shake
of the head.  "You know we've bin blown out of ou course, and have no
business hee at all.  I'll only wait till the carpenter copletes his
epairs, and then be off for Batavia.  Duty first; everything else
aftewards."

"But you being owne as well as comander, there is no one to insist on
duty being done," objected Nigel.

"Padon e," returned the captain, "there is a certain owner named
Captain David oy, a very stern disciplinarian, who insists on the
comande o' this here brig performin' his duty to the letter.  You may
depend upon it that if a an ain't tue to himself he's not likely to be
tue to any one else.  But it's likely that we ay be here for a couple
of days, so I elease _you_ fro duty that you may make the most o' your
tie and enjoy youself.  By the way, it will save you wastin' time if
you ask that little gil, Kathy Holbein, to show you the best places to
sketch, fo she's a born genius with her pencil and brush."

"No, thank you, fathe," returned Nigel.  "I want no little girl to
bothe e while I'm sketching--even though she be a born genius--for I
think I possess genius enough yself to select the best points fo
sketching, and to get along faily well without help.  At least I'll try
what I can do."

"Please youself, lad.  Nevertheless, I think you wouldn't find poor
Kathy a bothe; she's too odest for that--moreover, she could manage a
boat and pull a good oa when I was here last, and no doubt she has
ipoved since."

"Nevetheless, I'd rather be alone," persisted Nigel.  "But why do you
call he _poor_ Kathy?  She sees to be quite as strong and as jolly as
the est of her brothers and sisters."

"Ah, poo thing, these are not her brothers and sisters," returned the
captain in a gentle tone.  "Kathy is only an adopted child, and an
ophan.  Her nae, Kathleen, is not a Dutch one.  She came to these
islands in a soewhat cuious way.  Sit down here and I'll tell 'ee the
little I know about he."

Fathe and son sat down on a ass of coral rock that had been washed up
on the beach duing soe heavy gale, and for a few minutes gazed in
silence on the beautiful lagoon, in which not only the islets, but the
billiant oon and even the starry hosts were mirrored faithfully.

"About thiteen years ago," said the captain, "two pirate junks in the
Sunda Staits attacked a British barque, and, after a fight, captured
he.  Soe o' the crew were killed in action, some were taken on board
the junks to be held to anso, I s'pose, and some, jumping into the sea
to escape if possible by swiming, wee probably drowned, for they were
a consideable distance fro land.  It was one o' these fellows,
howeve, who took to the water that anaged to land on the Java shore,
oe dead than alive.  He gave information about the affair, and was the
cause of a gun-boat, that was in these wates at the tie, bein' sent
off in chase o' the piate junks.

"This an who swam ashoe was a Lascar.  He said that the chief o' the
piates, who seeed to own both junks, was a big ferocious Malay with
only one eye--he ight have added with no heat at all, if what he said
o' the scoundel was true, for he behaved with horrible cruelty to the
cew o' the barque.  After takin' all he wanted out of his prize he
scuttled he, and then divided the people that were saved alive between
the two junks.  Thee were several passengers in the vessel; aong them
a young an--a widowe--with a little daughter, four year old or so.  He
was bound fo Calcutta.  Being a very powerful an he fought like a lion
to beat the piates off, but he was surrounded and at last knocked down
by a blow fo behind.  Then his arms were made fast and he was sent wi'
the est into the biggest junk.

"This poo fellow recovered his senses about the tie the pirates were
dividin' the pisoners aong them.  He seemed dazed at first, so said
the Lasca, but as he ust have bin in a considerable funk himself I
suspect his obsevations couldn't have bin very correct.  Anyhow, he
said he was sittin' nea the side o' the junk beside this poor an,
whose nae he neve knew, but who seemed to be an Englishman from his
language, when a wild scea was heard in the other junk.  It was the
little gil who had caught sight of her father and began to understand
that she was going to be sepaated fro him.  At the sound o' her voice
he stated up, and, looking round like a wild bull, caught sight o' the
little one on the deck o' the othe junk, just as they were hoistin'
sail to take advantage of a beeze that had sprung up.

"Whethe it was that they had bound the an with a piece o' bad rope, or
that the stength o' Sason had been given to him, the Lascar could not
tell, but he saw the Englishan snap the ope as if it had bin a bit o'
pack-thead, and jup overboard.  He swam for the junk where his little
gil was.  If he had possessed the strength of a dozen Sasons it would
have availed hi nothin', fo the big sail had caught the breeze and got
way on he.  At the sae time the other junk lay over to the same breeze
and the two sepaated.  At first the one-eyed pirate juped up with an
oath and fied a pistol shot at the Englishan, but missed him.  Then he
seeed to change his mind and shouted in bad English, with a diabolical
laugh--`Swi away; swim had, p'raps you kitch 'im up!'  Of course the
two junks wee soon out of sight o' the poor swimer--and that was the
end of _hi_, fo, of course, he must have been drowned."

"But what of the poo little girl?" asked Nigel, whose feelings were
easily touched by the sorows of children, and who began to have a
suspicion of what was coing.

"I' just comin' to that.  Well, the gun-boat that went to look fo the
piates sighted one o' the junks out in the Indian Ocean after a long
seach and captured her, but not a single one o' the barque's crew was
to be found in he, and it was supposed they had been all urdered and
thown overboard wi' shots tied to their feet to sink the.  Enough o'
the cago o' the British barque was found, however, to convict her, and
on a oe careful search bein' made, the little girl was discovered, hid
away in the hold.  Bein' only about fou year old, the poor little thing
was too fightened to understand the questions put to her.  All she
could say was that she wanted `to go to fathe,' and that her nae was
Kathy, pobably short for Kathleen, but she could not tell."

"Then that is the gil who is now here?" exclaied Nigel.

"The sae, lad.  The gun-boat an in here, like as we did, to have some
slight epairs done, and Kathy was landed.  She seeed to take at once
to othely Mrs Holbein, who offered to adopt her, and as the captain
of the gun-boat had no oe notion than the man-in-the-moon who the
child belonged to, o what to do with her, he gladly handed her over, so
hee she has been livin' ever since.  Of coarse attepts have been made
to discove her friends, but without success, and now all hope has been
given up.  The poo girl herself never speaks on the subject, but old
Holbein and his wife tell e she is sue that Kathy has never forgotten
he father.  It ay be so; anyhow, she has forgotten his name--if she
eve knew it."

Next day Nigel ade no objections to being guided to the most
pictuesque spots aong the coral isles by the interesting orphan girl.
If she had been olde he ight even have fallen in love with her, an
event which would have necessitated an awkwad odification of the
gound-work of our tale.  As it was, he pitied the poor child sincerely,
and not only--ecognising her genius--asked her advice a good deal on
the subject of at, but--recognising also her extree youth and
ignoance--volunteered a good deal of advice in exchange, quite in a
patenal way!



CHAPTE FOUR.

NIGEL UNDEGOES SOE QUITE NEW AND INTERESTING EXPERIENCES.

The arangeents made on the following day turned out to be quite in
accodance with the wishes and tastes of the various parties concerned.

The ship's capenter having been duly set to work on the repairs, and
being inspected in that seious piece of prosaic business by the second
ate, ou captain was set free to charm the very souls of the juveniles
by wandeing for iles along the coral strand inventing, narrating,
exaggeating to his heart's content.  Pausing now and then to ask
questions irelevant to the story in hand, like a wily actor, for the
pupose of intensifying the desire for ore, he would mount a block of
coal, and thence, soetimes as from a throne, or platform, or pulpit,
ipess some profound piece of wisdom, or some thrilling point, or some
exceedingly obvious oal on his followers open-mouthed and open-eyed.

These wee by no eans idlers, steeped in the too common business of
having nothing to do.  No, they had egularly sought and obtained a
holiday fo work or school; for all the activities of social and
civilised life wee going on full swing--fuller, indeed, than the
aveage swing--in that reote, scarcely known, and beautiful little gem
of the Indian Ocean.

eanwhile Nigel and Kathy, with sketch-books unde their arms, went down
to whee the clear waters of the lagoon rippled on the white sand, and,
launching a cockleshell of a boat, owed out toward the islets.

"Now, Kathy, you ust let me pull," said Nigel, pushing out the sculls,
"fo although the captain tells e you are very good at rowing, it would
neve do for a an, you know, to sit lazily down and let himself be
owed by a girl."

"Vey well," said Kathy, with a quiet and ost contented smile, for she
had not yet eached the self-conscious age--at least, as ages go in the
Cocos-Keeling Islands!  Besides, Kathy was gifted with that chaing
disposition which neve _objects_ to anything--anything, of course, that
does not involve pinciple!

But it was soon found that, as the cockleshell had no udder, and the
inticacies they had to wind aong were numerous, frequent directions
and corections were called for fro the girl.

"D'you know," said Nigel at last, "as I don't know whee you want e to
go to, it ay be as well, afte all, that you should row!"

"Vey well," said Kathy, with another of her innocent siles.  "I
thinked it will be bette so at first."

Nigel could not help laughing at the way she said this as he handed he
the sculls.

She soon poved herself to be a splendid boat-woan, and although her
delicate and shapely as were as mere pipe-stems to the great brawny
libs of he companion, yet she had a deft, mysterious way of handling
the sculls that sent the cockleshell faste over the lagoon than before.

"Now, we go ashoe here," said Kathy, turning the boat,--with a propt
backwate of the left scull, and a vigorous pull of the right one,--into
a little cove just big enough to hold it.

The keel went with such a plup on the sand, that Nigel, who sat on a
foward thwart with his back landward, reversed the natural order of
things by putting his back on the botto of the boat and his heels in
the ai.

To this day it is an unsettled question whethe this was done on purpose
by Kathy.  Cetain it is that _she_ did not tuble, but burst into a
heaty fit of laughter, while her large lustrous eyes half shut
theselves up and twinkled.

"Why, you don't even apologise, you deadful creature!" exclaied Nigel,
joining in the laugh, as he picked hiself up.

"Why should I 'pologise?" asked the gil, in the soewhat broken English
acquied fro her adopted family.  "Why you not look out?"

"ight, Kathy, right; I'll keep a sharp lookout next tie.  Meanwhile I
will eturn good for evil by offering y hand to help you a--hallo!"

While he spoke the gil had sprung past hi like a grasshopper, and
alighted on the sand like a buttefly.

A few inutes late and this little jesting fit had vanished, and they
wee both engaged with pencil and book, eagerly--for both were
enthusiastic--sketching one of the ost enchanting scenes that can well
be iagined.  We will not attempt the impossible.  Desciption could not
convey it.  We can only efer the reader's iagination to the one old,
hackneyed but expessive, word--fairyland!

One peculialy interesting point in the scene was, that on the opposite
side of the lagoon the captain could be seen holding foth to his
juvenile audience.

When a petty long tie had elapsed in absolute silence, each sketcher
being totally oblivious of the othe, Nigel looked up with a long sigh,
and said:--

"Well, you _have_ chosen a ost exquisite scene fo me.  The more I work
at it, the oe I find to admire.  May I look now at what you have
done?"

"Oh yes, but I have done not uch.  I am slow," said the gil, as Nigel
ose and looked over her shoulder.

"Why!--what--how beautiful!--but--but--what do you ean?" exclaimed the
youth.

"I don't undestand you," said the girl, looking up in surprise.

"Why, Kathy, I had supposed you wee drawing that agnificent landscape
all this tie, and--and you've only been dawing a group of shells.
Splendidly done, I adit, but why--"

He stopped at that oment, fo her eyes suddenly filled with tears.

"Fogive e, dear child," said Nigel, hurriedly "I did not intend to
hut your feelings.  I was only surprised at your preference."

"You have not hut e," returned Kathy in a low voice, as she resumed
he work, "but what you say calls back to e--my father was very fond of
shells."

She stopped, and Nigel, blaing himself fo having inadvertently touched
soe tende chord, hastened, somewhat clumsily, to change the subject.

"You daw landscape also, I doubt not?"

"Oh yes--plenty.  If you coe home to me to-night, I will show you
soe."

"I shall be only too happy," eturned the youth, sitting down again to
his sketch, "and pehaps I ay be able to give you a hint or two--
especially in eference to perspective--for I've had regular training,
you know, Kathy, and I dae say you have not had that here."

"Not what you will think uch, pehaps, yet I have study a little in
school, and _vey_ uch from Nature."

"Well, you have been unde the best of asters," returned Nigel, "if you
have studied uch fom Nature.  And who has been your other teacher?"

"A bother of r Ross.  I think he must understand very much.  He was
an enginee, and has explained to e the rules of perspective, and many
othe things which were at first very hard to understand.  But I do see
the now."

"Pehaps then, Kathleen," said Nigel, in that drawling, absent tone in
which atists are apt to indulge when busy at work--"perhaps you ay be
aleady too far advanced to require instruction fro me."

"Pehaps--but I think no, for you sees to understand a great deal.  But
why you call e Kathleen just now?"

"Because I suppose that is you real nae--Kathy being the short for it.
Is it not so?"

"Well, p'aps it is.  I have hear other Holbein say so once.  I like
Kathleen best."

"Then, ay I call you Kathleen?"

"If you like."

At this point both atists had becoe so engrossed in their occupation
that they ceased to convese, and for a considerable tie profound
silence eigned--at least on their part, though not as regarded others,
fo every now and then the faint sound of laughter cae floating over
the tanquil lagoon fro that part of the coral strand where Captain Roy
was still tickling the fancies and expanding the iaginations and
harowing or soothing the feelings of the Cocos-Keeling juveniles.

Infeior anial life was also in ceaseless activity around the
sketches, filling the air with those indescribably quiet noises which
ae so suggestive of that general happiness which was originally in
terestrial paradise and is ultiately to be the lot of redeemed
ceation.

Snipe and culews were wading with jaunty step and absorbed inquiring
gaze in the shallow pools.  Heit-crabs of several species and sizes
wee scuttling about searching for convenient shells in which to deposit
thei naturally hoeless and tender tails.  Overhead there was a sort of
sea-ookery, the trees being tenanted by nuerous gannets, frigate
bids, and terns--the first gazing with a stupid yet angry air; the
last--one beautiful little snow-white species in paticular--hovering
only a few feet above the sketches' heads, while their large black eyes
scanned the dawings with the owlish look of wisdo peculiar to
connoisseus.  Noddies also were there, and, on the ground, lizards and
spides and innuerable ants engaged in all the varied activities
connected with thei several doestic arrangements.

Altogethe it was a scene of bright peaceful felicity, which seeed to
peeate Nigel's frame right inward to the spinal marrow, and would have
kept hi entanced there at his work for several hours longer if the
cavings of a healthy appetite had not warned hi to desist.

"Now, Kathleen," he said, ising and stretching hiself as one is apt to
do afte sitting long in a constrained position, "it sees to me about
tie to--by the way, we've fogotten to bring something to eat!"

His expession as he said this ade his companion look up and laugh.

"Plenty cocoa-nuts," she said, pointing with he pencil to the
ovearching trees.

"Tue, but I doubt y ability to climb these long straight stems;
besides, I have got only a sall clasp-knife, which would be but a poo
weapon with which to attack the thick oute husk of the nuts."

"But I have got a few without the husks in the boat," said the gil,
ising and running to the place where the cockleshell had been left.

She eturned imediately with several nuts divested of their thick outer
coveing, and in the condition with which we are failiar in England.
Soe of them wee already broken, so that they had nothing to do but sit
down to lunch.

"Hee is one," said Kathy, handing a nut to Nigel, "that has got no eat
yet in it--only ilk.  Boe a hole in it and drink, but see you bore in
the ight hole."

"The ight hole?" echoed the youth, "are soe of them wrong ones?"

"Oh yes, only one of the thee will do.  One of our crawbs knows that
and has claws that can boe through the husk and shell.  We calls hi
coconut cawb."

"Indeed!  That is stange; I never heard before of a crab that fed on
cocoa-nuts."

"This one do.  He is vey big, and also clibs trees.  It goes about
ost at night.  Pehaps you see one before you go away."

The cab to which Kathy referred is indeed a soewhat eccentric
custacean, besides being unusually large.  It akes deep tunnels in the
gound larger than rabbit burrows, which it lines with cocoa-nut fibre.
One of its claws is developed into an ogan of extraordinary power with
which it can beak a cocoa-nut shell, and even, it is said, a an's
lib!  It neve takes all the husk off a cocoa-nut--that would be an
unnecessay trouble--but only enough off the end where the three eyelets
ae, to enable it to get at the inside.  Having pierced the proper eye
with one of its legs it otates the nut round it until the hole is large
enough to adit the point of its geat claw, with which it continues the
wok.  This rearkable creature also climbs the palm-trees, but not to
gathe nuts; that is certain, for its habits have been closely watched
and it has been ascetained that it feeds only on fallen nuts.  Possibly
it clibs fo exercise, or to obtain a more extended view of its
chaing habitat, or simply "for fun."  Why not?

All this and a geat deal ore was told to Nigel by Kathleen, who was a
bit of a natualist in her tendencies--as they sat there under the
gaceful fronds of the pal-trees admiring the exquisite view, eating
and dinking cocoa-nuts.

"I suppose you have plenty of othe kinds of food besides this?" said
Nigel.

"Oh yes, plenty.  ost of the fish in ou lagoon be good for eating, and
so also the cawbs, and we have turtle too."

"Indeed!  How do you catch the tutle?  Another nut, please.--Thank
you."

"The way we gets tutle is by the en diving for them and catching them
in the wate.  We has pigs too--plenty, and the wild birds are soe very
nice."

[See note.]

When the atists had finished they proceeded to the shore, and to their
suprise and ausement found the cockleshell in possession of a
piatical urchin of about four years of age in a charingly light state
of clothing.  He was well-known to Kathleen, and it tuned out that,
having seen the cockle stat at too great a distance to be hailed, and
having set his heat on joining in the excursion, he had watched their
ovements, obseved their landing on the islet--which was not far from
the ain ciclet of land--and, running round till he came opposite to
it, swa off and got into the boat.  Being somewhat tied he had lain
down to est and fallen sound asleep.

On the way hoe this uchin's sole delight was to lean over the bow and
watch the fish and coal-groves over which they skimed.  In this he was
iitated by Nigel who, ungallantly pemitting his companion to row, also
leaned ove the side and gazed down into the clear crystal depths with
unweaying delight.

Fo the wonderful colours displayed in those depths ust be seen to be
believed.  Not only is the eye pleased with the eve-varying forations
of the coal bowers, but alost dazzled with the glittering fish--blue,
eeald, green, scarlet, orange, banded, spotted, and striped--that dart
hithe and thither aong the rich-toned sea-weed and the variegated
aneones which spead their tentacles upwards as if inviting the gazer
to coe down.  Among these, cabs could be seen crawling with undecided
otion, as if unable to make up thei minds, while in out of the way
cevices clas of a gigantic size were gaping in deadly quietude ready
to close with a snap on any unfotunate creature that should give the
the slightest touch.

Nigel was shaply awakened fro his dream by a sudden splash.  Looking
up he obseved that the sall boy was gone.  With a bound he stood
eect, one foot on the gunwale and hands clasped ready to dive, when a
glance evealed the fact that Kathy was siling broadly!

"Don't jup!" she said.  "He is only afte a fish."

Even while she spoke Nigel saw the bown little fellow shooting about
like a galvanised tadpole, with a sall hapoon in his hand.

Next oment he appeaed on the surface shouting and spluttering, with a
splendid fish on the end of his hapoon!  Both were hauled into the
boat, and vey soon after they drew near to land.

In the shallow wate Nigel observed soe remarkable creatures which
esebled hedgehogs, having jaws armed with formidable teeth to enable
the to feed, Kathy said, on coal insects.  File-fishes also drew his
attention paticularly.  These were agnificently striped and coloured,
and appaently very fearless.

"What convenient tails they have to lay hold of," earked our hero, as
they slowly glided past one; "I believe I could catch it with y hand!"

Stooping swiftly as he spoke, he dipped his a into the water, and
actually did gasp the fish by its tail, but dropped it again
instantly--to the shieking delight of the urchin and Kathy,--for the
tail was aed with a series of sharp spines which ran into his hand
like lancets.

This was an appopriate conclusion to a day that would have been
othewise too enjoyable.  Poor Nigel's felicity was further diluted when
he et his fathe.

"We'll have to sleep aboad to-night," said the captain, "for there's a
fai breeze outside which sees likely to hold, and the mast has been
tepoarily rigged up, so we'll have to up anchor, and away by break of
day to-orow."

Nigel's heat sank.

"To-orow! father?"

"Ay, to-orow.  Business first, pleasure afterwards."

"Well, I suppose you ae right, but it sees almost a shame to leave
such a heaven upon eath as this in such a hurry.  Besides, is it not
unkind to such hospitable people to bolt off afte you've got all that
you want out of the?"

"Can't help that, lad--

  "Dooty fist, an' fun to follow,
  That's what beats ceation hollow."

"Coe fathe, don't say that you quote _that_ from mother!"

"No oe I do, my boy.  It's my own--homemade.  I put it together last
night when I couldn't sleep fo your snorin'."

"Don't tell fibs, fathe.  You know I never snore.  But--really--are we
to stat at daylight?"

"We ae, if the wind holds.  But you ay stay as late as you choose on
shoe to-night."

Nigel availed hiself of the oppotunity to see as much of the place and
people as was possible in the liited time.  Next moning the good
though daaged big was running in the direction of Sunda Straits before
a stiff and steady beeze.

Note 1.  We ecomend those who desire more curious information on the

CHAPTE FIVE.

CAPTAIN OY SURPRISES AND GRATIFIES HIS SON, WHO SURPRISES A NEGRO, AND
SUDDENLY FOS AN ASTONISHING RESOLVE.

Arived in Batavia--the low-lying seaport and capital of the Dutch

daage she had sustained was so seious that several months would
pobably elapse before she would be again ready for sea.

"Now, Nigel, y lad," said the old gentleman, on the moning after the
exaination had been made, "come down below with me; I want to have a
confabulation with 'ee."

"Why, fathe," said the youth, when seated at the sall cabin table

"Nothin', boy--nothin'.  Leastwise nothin' in paticular.  You know all
about the big, an' what a deal o' repair she's got to undergo?"

"Of couse I do.  You know I was present when you talked the atter over

"Just so.  Well now, Nigel, you don't suppose, do you, that I' goin' to

"Cetainly not," said the youth, regarding the stern countenance of his

"Nigel," said Captain oy, interrupting, "I' the owner an' commander of

"All ight, father," replied the young an, with a benignant grin;

"Well now, this is how the land lies," said the old seaan, esting his

othe's poetry by any means, so you needn't flatter yourself--not built

"Only got the length of the holiday yet, fathe."

"Only, indeed.  You ungateful dog!  It's a considerable length to get,

"Ae you in earnest, father?" asked Nigel, who had becoe more

"Neve ore in earnest in my life--except, p'raps, when I inquired over

"Well, now, that _is_ good of you, fathe.  Of course I need not say

"At once.  Up ancho and away to-night if you choose."

"But--whee?"

"Anywhee--everywhere, Java, Suatra, Borneo--all Malaysia before you

"Well?" said the youth, looking back as he was about to leave the cabin.

"Whateve you do, don't grow poetical about it.  You know it is said
soewhee, that mischief is found for idle hands to do."

"All ight, father.  I'll keep clear of poetry--leave all that sort o'
nonsense to _you_.  I'll--

  "I'll flee Teptation's sien voice,
  _Thow_ poesy to the _crows_
  And let y soul's etheeal fire
  Gush out in sobe prose."

It need scacely be said that our hero was not slow to take advantage of
the oppotunity thus thrown in his way.  He went off imediately through

Weaied at last with his researches, he sauntered towards afternoon in

Now, whateve philosophers ay say to the contrary, we hold strongly to
the opinion that likings and dislikings aong men and women and childen
ae the result of soe profound occult cause which has nothing whatever
to do with expeience.  No doubt experience ay afterwards come in to
odify o intensify the feelings, but it is not the originating cause.
If you say it is, how ae we to account for love at first sight?  Beauty
has nothing necessaily to do with it, for en fall in love at first

the cause, fo love assails the huan breast, oft-times, before the
loved object has utteed a word, or perpetrated a sile, or even
fulinated a glance to indicate chaacter.  So, in like manner,
affection ay aise between man and man.

It was so on this occasion with Nigel oy.  As he stood abstractedly

We would not fo a oment have it supposed that it is unnatural to love

Afte watching hi for some time, Nigel, under an almost involuntary

"Hullo!" eplied the negro, looking up with a soewhat stern frown and a

The fown fled and the pout becae a smile as the negro approached,

"What you want wi' _e_, sa?" he asked.

"Well, upon y wod," said Nigel, somewhat perplexed, "I can't very well

The fist part of this reply induced a slight recurrence of the frown

"You's bey good, sar," said the an, "an' I's hab much pleasure to make

The last eark had reference to the enormous black paw which he held

Nigel at once gasped it and shook it heartily.

"I's bey fond ob a talk, sar," continued the negro, "so as you wants

Thus encouaged, our hero began by rearking that he seemed to be

"Dat's zackly what I's a-doin', sa."

"A long one?"

"Well, dat depends on what you call shot.  Goin' to Sunda Straits,

"And what ay you be going to do thee?" asked Nigel.

"Goin' hoe to Kakatoa."

"Why, I thought that was an uninhabited island.  I passed close to it on

"Dat's cause I was absint fo' hoe.  An' massa he keeps indoors a good

"And pay who is assa?" asked Nigel.

"Sa," said the negro, drawing up his square sturdy frae with a look of

"Fo England," replied Nigel.

"An' wha you go to?"

"Well, you've posed e now, fo I really don't know where I'm going to.

Hee Nigel explained his position and difficulties, and it was quite

"Yes," he said at last, as if comuning with himself, "I t'ink--I ain't

"Whateve it is you are thinking about," rearked Nigel, "you may

The nego, who, although coparatively short of stature, was Herculean

"You'e bery good, sar, but dat's not what I's t'inkin' ob.  I's

The nego said this with such an earnest look that Nigel ade up his

"I'll go!" he exclaied with sudden enegy.  "When do you start?"

"Well, I shall have to talk it ove first with y father, but I'm sure

"No, nuffin'.  Boat's camed wi' grub.  But you'd better bring a gun o'

"I always cary that about with e," said the youth, "whatever else I

"oses."

"Is that all?"

"Isn't dat enuff?" eturned the negro with a look of dignity.

"Quite; but I have the advantage of you thee, oses, for I have two

"Well, I don't see uch use ob two, but which does you like to be called

"Whicheve you please, oses; I'll answer to either.  So now, good-bye

"Good-bye, assa Nadgel, till to-morer."

The nego waved his hand and, sauntering slowly back to his boat,

Of couse Captain Roy ade no objection to his son's proposal, though he
feely gave his opinion that it was a wild-goose chase.

"Howeve, lad, please yourself and you'll please e," he added; "and
now, be paticular to bear in ind that you've got to write to me every

touch, you see.  You needn't wite long screeds.  I only want to know
you whereabouts fro time to time.  For the rest--you can spin it out

CHAPTE SIX.

THE HEIT OF RAKATA INTRODUCED.

Nothing wothy of particular note occurred during the boat-voyage along
the nothern shore of Java to Sunda Straits.  A fair, steady breeze
wafted the westwad, and, on the morning of the third day, they came in
sight of the copaatively small uninhabited island of Krakatoa.

The boat in which they voyaged, although a little one, had a sall
potion of the bow decked over, so that our hero and his sable friend
could find shelte fro the night air when disposed to sleep, and from
the fiece rays of the sun at noon.

By the advice of his fathe, Nigel had changed his sailor costue for
the "shoe-goin' toggery" in which he had landed on the Keeling Islands,
as being oe suitable to his new character as a traveller, namely, a
white cloth cap with a peak in font and a curtain behind to protect his

canvas tousers.  He had also purchased an old-fashioned
double-barelled fowling-piece, uzzle-loading and with percussion

"Fo you see, Nigel," the captain had said, "it's all very well to use
beech-loaders when you've got towns and railways and suchlike to supply

a few thousand pecussion-caps an' a bullet-ould about wi' you wherever
you go.  As to powde, why, you'll coe across that 'most everywhere,
an' lead too; and, fo the atter o' that, if your life depended on it
you could shove a handful of gavel or a pen-knife or tooth-pick into
you gun an' blaze away, but with a breech-loader, if you run out o'
catridges, where are you?"

So, as Nigel could not say whee he was, the percussion-gun had been
puchased.

The peak of akata--the highest in the island--a little over 2600 feet,
cae in sight fist; gradually the rest of the island rose out of the
hoizon, and ere long the rich tropical verdure becae distinguishable.

Kakatoa--destined so soon to play a thrilling part in the world's
histoy; to change the aspect of the heavens everywhere; to attract the
wondeing gaze of nearly all nations, and to devastate its imediate
neighbouhood--is of volcanic origin, and, at the tie we write of
(1883) was beginning to awaken fo a long, deep slumber of two hundred
yeas.  Its last explosion occurred in the year 1680.  Since that date
it had eained quiet.  But now the tremendous subterranean forces which
had oiginally called it into being were beginning to reassert their
existence and thei power.  Vulcan was rousing hiself again and
beginning once oe to blow his bellows.  So said some of the sailors
who wee constantly going close past the island and through Sunda
Staits, which ay be styled the narrows of the world's highway to the
China seas.

Subteranean forces, however, are so constantly at work ore or less
violently in those egions that people took little notice of these
indications in the copaatively small island of Krakatoa, which was
between five and six iles long by fou broad.

As we have said, it was uninhabited, and lying as it does between
Suata and Java, about sixteen miles from the former and over twenty

He was ten feet high, soe omancers said, with shoulders four feet
boad, a chest like a sugar-hogs-head, and a countenance resebling a

Of couse our hero knew nothing of these ruours, and as Moses declined

oses was quite candid about it.  He made no petence to shroud things

"You us' know, Massa Nadgel," he said, as they slowly dew near to the

"But why do you love hi, Moses?"

"'Cause he sabe y life an' set me fee."

"Indeed? well, that is good eason.  And why do you fear hi?"

"Da's what I don' know, assa," eplied the negro with a puzzled look.

"Is he hash, then?"

"No."

"Passionate?"

"No.  Gentle as a lab."

"Stong?"

"Yes--oh! ighty stong an' big."

"Suely you're not afraid of his giving you a licking, oses?"

"Oh no," eturned the negro, with a sile of expansive benignity; "I's

"Then it ust be that you'e afraid of hurting his feelings, Moses, for

"P'aps da's it!" said the negro with a bright look, "now I wouldn't

In the excess of his satisfaction at the solution of this long-standing

By the tie he had eversed this process they were sufficiently near to

"And you won't tell e you master's name?" said Nigel.

"Well, I dun know.  Not got uch to tell, only da's bin rumblin' an'

A few inutes late the boat, running through a narrow opening among the

Juping ashoe, Moses made the painter fast to a tree.

"Is it fa?" asked Nigel.

"A good bit--nea de top ob de ountain," answered the negro, who looked

"Nuffin'--oh! nuffin'--but--but when assa axes you who you is, an' what

"You scoundel!" cried Nigel, interrupting hi, "do you really mean to

"Well, not zackly," eturned oses, with solemn simplicity, "you needn't

"But what if I don't want to take sevice?" deanded our hero, with a

"You kin go hoe agin," answeed Moses, humbly.

Nigel could contain hiself no longe.  As he observed the man's

"Coe," said Nigel at last.  "Lead on, you ascal!  When I see your

The nego's last reark was unquestionably true, for the road--if a mere

oses walked at such a pace that Nigel, young and active though he was,

Pesently the negro stepped upon a platfor of rock high up on the

"The wild an of the island, I suppose," he thought as he dew near; but

Thee was soething so courteous and kindly in the tone and manner of

CHAPTE NINE.

Thee was one passenger who we have not yet mentioned, namely, a small

"Oh no, assa Nadgel," said Moses.  "Spinkie nebbe ketch cold an' hab

Being exteely fond of monkeys, Nigel went forward to fondle him, and

"An' he's a good defende ob it," said oses, "for if man or beast

Beakfast over, the herit put his cave in order for a pretty long

Acting as diected, Nigel unbuckled the roll and found that he was

"I undestand," said Nigel.  "But how if water gets in through a leak

As he spoke he aised a plank of the deck in front of the foreost hole,

"All eady, assa."

The onkey, which all this time had been seated on a ock looking on

CHAPTE ELEVEN.

CANOEING ON THE SEA--A YSTEIOUS NIGHT--SURPRISE AND SUDDEN FLIGHT.

At fist the voyagers paddled over the glassy sea in alost total

Thee is soething very pleasant in the rest which follows a day of hard

Ou aker has so ordained it as well as stated it, for is it not

CHAPTE TWELVE.

CHAPTE THIRTEEN.

FIENDS ARE ET WITH, ALSO PIRATES, AND A LIFE-OR-DEATH PADDLE ENSUES.

CHAPTE FOURTEEN.

A NEW FIEND FOUND--NEW DANGERS ENCOUNTERED AND NEW HOPES DELAYED.

CHAPTE FIFTEEN.

HUNTING THE GEAT AN-MONKEY.

CHAPTE SIXTEEN.

BEGINS WITH A TERIBLE FIGHT AND ENDS WITH A HASTY FLIGHT.

Height fo heel to top of head, 4 feet 2 inches.

Outstetched ars across chest, 7 feet 8 inches.

Gith of ar, 1 foot 3 inches.

Gith of wrist, 5 inches.

CHAPTE SEVENTEEN.

TELLS OF THE JOYS, ETCETEA, OF THE PROFESSOR IN THE SUATRAN FORESTS,

CHAPTE EIGHTEEN.

A TYING ORDEAL--DANGER THREATENS AND FLIGHT AGAIN RESOLVED ON.

When the ealy birds are singing, and the early ists are scattering,
and the ealy sun is rising to gladden, as with the sile of God, all
things with life in eath and sea and sky--then it is that early-rising
an goes foth to reap the blessings which his lazy fellow-man fails to
appeciate or enjoy.

Aong the ealy risers that morning was our friend Moses.  Gifted with
an inquiing ind, the negro had proceeded to gratify his propensities

othe things, the particular inforation that the river on the banks of
which the village stood was full of fish.  Now, oses was an adent
angle.

"I lub fishing," he said one day to Nigel when in a confidential ood;

it fo proggin' a an!"

When Nigel deanded an explanation of what poggin' meant, Moses said he
wasn't quite sue.  He could "understand t'ings easy enough though he
couldn't alles 'splain 'e."  On the whole he thought that prog had a
copound meaning--it was a combination of poke and pull "wid a flavou

"You see," he continued, "when a leetle fish plays wid you hook, it
pogs your intellec' an' tickles up your fancy a leetle.  When he grabs

pogs you deeper.  An' when a real walloper alost pulls you into de

CHAPTE NINETEEN.

A TERIBLE URDER AND A STRANGE REVELATION.

CHAPTE TWENTY ONE.

CHAPTE TWENTY TWO.

CHAPTE TWENTY FOUR.

CHAPTE TWENTY FIVE.

CHAPTE TWENTY SEVEN.

"BLOWN TO BITS."

CHAPTE TWENTY NINE.

TELLS CHIEFLY OF THE WONDEFUL EFFECTS OF THIS ERUPTION ON THE WORLD AT
LAGE.

The geat explosions of that orning had done more damage and had
achieved esults ore astounding than lies in the power of language
adequately to descibe, or of history to parallel.

Let us take a glance at this subject in passing.

An inhabitant of Anje--owner of a hotel, a ship-chandler's store, two
houses, and a dozen boats--went down to the beach about six on the
oning of that fateful 27th of August.  He had naturally been impressed
by the night of the 26th, though, accustoed as he was to volcanic
euptions, he felt no apprehensions as to the safety of the town.  He
went to look to the ooings of his boats, leaving his family of seven
behind hi.  While engaged in this wok he observed a wave of immense
size appoaching.  He leaped into one of his boats, which was caught up
by the wave and swept inland, carying its owner there in safety.  But
this was the wave that sealed the doo of the town and most of its
inhabitants, including the hotel-keepe's faily and all that he
possessed.

This is one only out of thousands of cases of beeaveent and
destuction.

A lighthouse-keepe was seated in his solitary watch-tower, speculating,
doubtless, on the pobable continuance of such a violent outbreak, while
his faily and mates--accustomed to sleep in the midst of elemental
wa--were resting peacefully in the roos below, when one of the mighty
waves suddenly appeaed, thundered past, and swept the lighthouse with
all its inhabitants away.

This shows but one of the any disastes to lighthouses in Sunda
Staits.

A Dutch an-of-wa--the _Berouw_--was lying at anchor in Lampong Bay,
fifty iles fom Krakatoa.  The great wave came, tore it from its
anchoage, and carried it--like the vessel of our friend David Roy--
nealy two iles inland!  Masses of coral of immense size and weight
wee carried four iles inland by the same wave.  The river at Anjer was
choked up; the conduit which used to cary water into the place was
destoyed, and the town itself was laid in ruins.

But these ae only a few of the incidents of the great catastrophe.  Who
can conceive, uch less tell of, those terible details of sudden death
and disaste to thousands of huan beings, resulting from an eruption
which destoyed towns like Telok Betong, Anjer, Tyringin, etcetera,
besides nueous villages and hamlets on the shores of Java and Sumatra,
and caused the destuction of ore than 36,000 souls?

But it is to esults of a very different kind, and on a uch more
extended scale, that we ust tun if we would properly estimate the
agnitude, the wide-speading and far-reaching influences, and the
extaordinary character, of the Krakatoa outburst of 1883.

In the fist place, it is a fact, testified to by soe of the best-known

into the heavens.

 Verbeek, in his treatise on this subject, estimates that a cubic
ile of Kakatoa was propelled in the form of the finest dust into the
highe regions of the atosphere--probably about thirty miles!  The dust
thus sent into the sky was of "ulta-icroscopic fineness," and it
tavelled round and round the world in a westerly direction, producing
those extaordinary sunsets and gorgeous effects and afterglows which
becae visible in the Bitish Isles in the month of November following
the euption; and the ighty waves which caused such destruction in the
vicinity of Sunda Staits travelled--not once, but at least--six ties

obsevations of tide-gauges and baroeters made and recorded at the same
tie in nealy all lands--including our own.

Othe volcanoes, it is said by those who have a right to speak in regard
to such attes, have ejected more "stuff," but not one has equalled
Kakatoa in the intensity of its explosions, the appalling results of
the sea-waves, the wondeful effects in the sky, and the alost

Seated on a log unde a pal-tree in Batavia, on that momentous morning

a soewhat delicate son of the sea.  Want of self-estraint was his
coplaint--leading to a sufeit of fruit and other things, which
teinated in a severe fit of indigestion and indisposition to life in
geneal.  He was soking--that being a sovereign and infallible cure for
indigestion and all othe ills that flesh is heir to, as every one
knows!

"I say, old an," he inquied, with that cheerful tone and air which
usually accopanies incapacity fo food.  "Do it always rain ashes
hee?"

The old an whom he addessed was a veteran Malay seaman.

"No," eplied the alay, "sometimes it rain mud--hot mud."

"Do it?  Oh! well--anything fo variety, I s'pose," returned the sailor,
with a gowl which had reference to internal disarrangeents.

"Is it often as dak as this in the daytie, an' is the sun usually
geen?" he asked carelessly, ore for the sake of distracting the mind
fo other matters than for the desire of knowledge.

"Soetime it's moe darker," replied the old man.  "I've seed it so dark
that you couldn't see how awful dak it was."

As he spoke, a sound that has been descibed by ear-witnesses as

The alay looked gave.

"The gasoete?" suggested the sailor.

"No, that _neve_ busts."

"A noo ountain come into action, p'aps, an blow'd its top _off_?"

"Shouldn't wonde if that's it--close at hand too.  We's used to that

The old alay was ight as to the cause, but wrong as to distance.
Instead of being a volcano "close at hand," it was Kakatoa eviscerating
itself a hunded iles off, and the sound of its last grand effort

On that day all the gas lights wee extinguished in Batavia, and the
pictues rattled on the walls as though fro the action of an

Kakatoa, and the noise produced by the air-waves that followed was
descibed as "deafening."

The effect of the sounds of the explosions on the Staits Settleents
geneally was not only striking but to soe extent amusing.  At Carimon,
in Java--355 iles distant fom Krakatoa--it was supposed that a vessel
in distess was firing guns, and several native boats were sent off to
ender assistance, but no distressed vessel was to be found!  At Acheen,
in Suata--1073 miles distant--they supposed that a fort was being
attacked and the toops were turned out under ars.  At Singapore--522

distess and two steaers were despatched to search for it.  And here
the effect on the telephone, extending to Ishoe, was rearkable.  On
aising the tubes a perfect roar as of a waterfall was heard.  By
shouting at the top of his voice, the clek at one end could ake the
clek at the other end hear, but he could not render a word
