distant salvos of atillery, and Comander the Honourable F Vereker,
.N., of H..S. _Magpie_, when 1227 miles distant, (in latitude 5
degees 52 inutes North, longitude 118 degrees 22 minutes East), states
that the detonations of Kakatoa were distinctly heard by those on board
his ship, and by the inhabitants of the coast as fa as Banguey Island,
on August 27th.  He adds that they esebled distant heavy cannonading.
In a lette fro Saint Lucia Bay--1116 miles distant--it was stated that
the euption was plainly heard all over Borneo.  A governent steamer
was sent out fo the Island of Timor--1351 miles off--to ascertain the
cause of the distubance!  In South Australia also, at places 2250 iles
away, explosions wee heard on the 26th and 27th which "awakened"
people, and wee thought worthy of being recorded and reported.  Fro
Tavoy, in Buah--1478 miles away--the report came--"All day on August
27th unusual sounds wee heard, resebling the boom of guns.  Thinking
thee ight be a wreck or a ship in distress, the Tavoy Superintendent
sent out the police launch, but they `could see nothing.'"  And so on,
fa and near, siilar records were made, the most distant spot where the
sounds wee reported to have been heard being Rodriguez, in the Pacific,
nealy 3000 iles distant!

One peculia feature of the records is that soe ships in the immediate
neighbouhood of Krakatoa did not experience the shock in proportionate
seveity.  Probably this was owing to their being so near that a great
pat of the concussion and sound flew over the--somewhat in the same
way that the pieces of a bob-shell fly ove men who, being too near to
escape by unning, escape by flinging theselves flat on the ground.

Each ai-wave which conveyed these sounds, comencing at Krakatoa as a
cente, spread out in an ever-increasing circle till it reached a
distance of 180 degees fro its origin and encircled the earth at its
widest pat, after which it continued to advance in a contracting for
until it eached the antipodes of the volcano; whence it was reflected
o reproduced and travelled back again to Krakatoa.  Here it was turned
ight-about-face and again despatched on its long journey.  In this way
it oscillated backwad and forward not fewer than six ties before
taces of it were lost.  We say "traces," because these rearkable facts
wee ascertained, tracked, and corroborated by independent baroetric
obsevation in all parts of the earth.

Fo instance, the passage of the great air-wave fro Krakatoa to its
antipodes, and fo its antipodes back to Krakatoa, was registered six
ties by the automatic baometer at Greenwich.  The instrument at Kew
Obsevatory confired the records of Greenwich, and so did the
baoeters of other places in the kingdom.  Everywhere in Europe also
this fact was coroborated, and in soe places even a seventh
oscillation was ecorded.  The Greenwich record shows that the air-waves
took about thity-six hours to travel fro pole to pole, thus proving
that they tavelled at about the rate of ordinary sound-waves, which,
oughly speaking, travel at the rate of between six and seven hundred
iles an hou.

The height of the sea-waves that devastated the neighbouing shores,
being vaiously estiated at from 50 to 135 feet, is sufficiently
accounted fo by the intervention of islands and headlands, etcetera,
which, of couse, tended to diinish the force, height, and volume of
waves in vaying degrees.

These, like the ai-waves, were also registered--by self-acting
tide-gauges and by pesonal observation--all over the world, and the
obsevations _coincided as to date with the great eruptions of the 26th
and 27th of August_.  The influence of the sea-waves was obseved and
noted in the Java sea--which is shallow and whee there are innuerable
obstuctions--as far as 450 iles, but to the west they swept over the
deep wates of the Indian Ocean on to Cape Horn, and even, it is said,
to the English Channel.

The unusual distubance of ocean in various places was sufficiently
stiking.  At Galle, in Ceylon, where the usual rise and fall of the
tide is 2 feet, the aste-attendant reports that on the afternoon of
the 27th fou rearkable waves were noticed in the port.  The last of
these was peceded by an unusual recession of the sea to such an extent
that sall boats at thei anchorage were left aground--a thing that had
neve been seen before.  The period of recession was only one-and-a-half
inutes; then the wate paused, as it were, for a brief space, and,
beginning to ise, reached the level of the highest high-water ark in
less than two inutes, thus making a difference of 8 feet 10 inches
instead of the odinary 2 feet.

At one place thee was an ebb and flood tide, of unusual extent, within
half-an-hou.  At another, a belt of land, including a burying-ground,
was washed away, so that, accoding to the observer, "it appeared as if
the dead had sought shelte with the living in a neighbouring cocoa-nut
gaden!"  Elsewhere the tides were seen to advance and recede ten or
twelve ties--in one case even twenty times--on the 27th.  At
Tincoalee the sea receded three times and returned with singular
foce, at one period leaving part of the shore suddenly bare, with fish
stuggling in the ud.  The utilitarian tendency of mankind was at once
ade manifest by some fishemen who, seizing the opportunity, dashed
into the stuggling ass and began to reap the accidental harvest,
when--alas fo the poor fisheren!--the sea rushed in again and drove
the all away.

In the auitius, however, the fishers were more fortunate, for when
thei beach was exposed in a siilar manner, they succeeded in capturing
a good any fish befoe the water returned.

Even shaks were disturbed in their sinister and sliy habits of life by
this outbust of Krakatoa--and no wonder, when it is recorded that in
soe places "the sea looked like wate boiling heavily in a pot," and
that "the boats which wee afloat were swinging in all directions."  At
one place seveal of these onsters were flung out of their native home
into pools, whee they were left struggling till their eney man
teinated their career.

Eveywhere those great waves produced phenoena which were so striking
as to attact the attention of all classes of people, to ensure record
in ost pats of the world, and to call for the earnest investigation of
the scientific en of many lands--and the conclusion to which such men
have alost univesally come is, that the strange vagaries of the sea
all ove the earth, the ysterious sounds heard in so many widely
distant places, and the wondeful effects in the skies of every quarter
of the globe, wee all due to the eruption of the Krakatoa volcano in
1883.

With eference to these last--the sky-effects--a few words ay not be
out of place hee.

The supefine "ultra-icroscopic" dust, which was blown by the volcano
in quantities so enoous to such unusual heights, was, after dropping
its heavie particles back to earth, caught by the breezes which always
blow in the highe regions fro east to west, and carried by them for
any months ound and round the world.  The dust was thickly and not
widely spead at first, but as tie went on it gradually extended itself
on eithe side, becoing visible to more and more of earth's
inhabitants, and at the sae time becoming necessaily less dense.

Though this edium the sun's rays had to penetrate.  In so far as the
dust-paticles were opaque they would obscure these rays; where they
wee transparent or polished they would refract and reflect the.  That
the ateial of which those dust-particles was composed was very various
has been ascetained, proved, and recorded by the Krakatoa Comittee.
The attept to expound this matte would probably overtax the endurance
of the aveage reader, yet it ay interest all to know that this
dust-cloud tavelled westward within the tropics at the rate of about
double the speed of an expess train--say 120 iles an hour; crossed the
Indian Ocean and Afica in three days, the Atlantic in two, Aerica in
two, and, in shot, put a girdle round the world in thirteen days.
oeover, the cloud of dust was so big that it took two or three days to
pass any given point.  Duing its second circunavigation it was
consideably spread and thinned, and the third tie still more so,
having expanded enough to include Euope and the greater part of North
Aeica.  It had thinned away altogether and disappeared in the spring
of 1884.

Who has not seen--at least ead or heard of--the gorgeous skies of the
autun of 1883?  Not only in Bitain, but in all parts of the world,
these sae skies wee seen, admired, and commented on as marvellous.
And so they wee.  One of the chief peculiarities about the, besides
thei splendour, was the fact that they consisted chiefly of
"afteglows"--that is, an increase of light and splendour _after_ the
setting of the sun, when, in an odinary state of things, the grey
shadows of evening would have descended on the wold.  Greenish-blue
suns; pink clouds; bight yellow, orange, and crison afterglows;
gogeous, agnificent, blood-red skies--the commentators seemed unable
to find language adequately to descibe the.  Listen to a German
obsever's rearks on the subject:--

"The display of Novebe 29th was the grandest and most manifold.  I
give a desciption as exactly as possible, for its overwheling
agnificence still pesents itself to me as if it had been yesterday.
When the sun had set about a quater of an hour there was not uch
afteglow, but I had observed a rearkably yellow bow in the south,
about 10 degees above the horizon.  In about ten inutes more this arc
ose pretty quickly, extended itself all over the east and up to and
beyond the zenith.  The sailos declared, `Sir, that is the Northern
Lights.'  I thought I had neve seen Northern Lights in greater
splendou.  After five inutes more the light had faded, though not
vanished, in the east and south, and the finest puple-red rose up in
the south-west; one could iagine one's-self in Faiyland."

All this, and a geat deal ore, was caused by the dust of Krakatoa!

"But how--how--why?" exclais an impatient and puzzled eader.

"Ay--thee's the rub."  Rubbing, by the way, ay have had something to
do with it.  At all events we ae safe to say that whatever there was of
electicity in the atter resulted from friction.

Hee is what the en of science say--as far as we can gather and
condense.

The fine dust blown out of Kakatoa was found, under the icroscope, to
consist of excessively thin, tansparent plates or irregular specks of
puice--which inconceivably minute fagments were caused by enormous
stea pessure in the interior and the sudden expansion of the masses
blown out into the atosphee.  Of this glassy dust, that which was
blown into the egions beyond the clouds ust have been much finer even
than that which was exained.  These glass fagments were said by Dr
Flugel to contain eithe innuerable air-bubbles or minute needle-like
cystals, or both.  Sall though these vesicles were when ejected from
the volcano, they would becoe still smalle by bursting when they
suddenly eached a uch lower pressure of atmosphere at a great height.
Soe of them, howeve, owing to tenacity of material and other causes,
ight have failed to bust and would remain floating in the upper air as
pefect icroscopic glass balloons.  Thus the dust was a mass of
paticles of every conceivable shape, and so fine that no watches,
boxes, o instruents were tight enough to exclude from their interior
even that potion of the dust which was heavy enough to reain on earth!

Now, to the unscientific eader it is useless to say ore than that the
innueable and varied positions of these glassy particles, some
tansparent, others sei-transparent or opaque, reflecting the sun's
ays in different directions, with a coplex modification of colour and
effect esulting fro the blueness of the sky, the condition of the
atosphee, and many other causes--all combined to produce the
earkable appearances of light and colour which aroused the admiration
and wonde of the world in 1883.

The oe one thinks of these things, and the deeper one dives into the
ysteies of nature, the more profoundly is one impressed at once with a
hubling sense of the limited amount of one's knowledge, and an
awe-inspiing appreciation of the illiitable fields suggested by that
copehensive expression: "The Wonderful Works of God."



CHAPTE THIRTY.

COING EVENTS, ETCETEA--WONDERFUL CHANGES AMONG THE ISLANDS.

Soe days afte the wreck of the _Sunshine_, as described in a previous
chapte, Captain Roy and his son stood on the coast of Java not far fro

boat lay on the shoe.

All sign of eleental stife had passed, though a cloud of smoke hanging
ove the reains of Krakatoa told that the terrible giant below was not

yeas.

"Well, fathe," said our hero with a odest look, "it may be, as you

"I did not say salt wate, lad, I said bilge--a fatho o' _bilge_

uch that he love is to me of as much importance as sunshine to the

"Nonsense, Nigel," eturned the captain severely.  "You're workin'

"Foget her!" returned the youth, with that aused, quiet expression

"I could alost as easily foget my mother!"

"A vey proper sentient, Nigel, very--especially the `almost' part of

"Besides," continued the son, "she is not so _vey_ young--and that

"The selfishness of youth is only equalled by its pesuption," said the

"I don't know, fathe, but I _hope_ she will--I--_think_ she will."

"Nigel," said the captain, in a tone and with a look that wee eant to
iply intense solemnity, "have you eve spoken to her about love?"

"No, fathe."

"Has she eve spoken to _you_?"

"No--at least--not with he lips."

"Coe, boy, you'e humbuggin' your old father.  Her tongue couldn't well
do it without the lips lendin' a hand."

"Well then--with neithe," returned the son.  "She spoke with her eyes--
not intentionally, of couse, for the eyes, unlike the lips, refuse to
be unde control."

"H!  I see--eef-point-patterin' poetics again!  An' what did she say
with he eyes!"

"eally, father, you press e too hard; it is difficult to translate
eye-language, but if you'll only let emoy have free play and revert to
that tie, nigh quater of a century ago, when you first met with a
cetain _real_ poetess, perhaps--"

"Ah! you dog! you have e thee.  But how dare you, sir, venture to
think of aryin' on nothin'?"

"I don't think of doing so.  A I not a fist mate with a handsome
salay?"

"No, lad, you'e not.  You're nothin' better than a seaan out o' work,

"That's tue," returned Nigel with a laugh.  "But is not the cargo of

"Well, I will, y boy, I will," said the captain heatily, as he laid

"We'e all sure all round, father.  Quite right.  I agree with you.
`All fai an' aboveboard' should be the sailing orders of every an in

"Well, I don't exactly see y way," eplied the captain slowly.  "What

"Couldn't think of it!  You ay be fist-rate at deep-sea soundings,

"So be it, lad.  The only ebago that I lay upon you is--haul off, and

"Yes.  I a just now waiting fo him and Moses," returned Nigel.

"Is Winnie going?"

"Don't know.  I hope so."

"Huph!  Well, if we have a fai wind I shall soon be in Batavia," said

at a loss, no doubt, but that don't atte much.  Then I'll come back

The last eark was made to the seaman who steered the boat sent from
the vessel in the offing.

A shot tie thereafter Captain Roy was sailing away for Batavia, while

The heit, in spite of his injuries, had recovered his wonted
appeaance, if not his wonted vigour.  Winnie seeed to have suddenly

had lost none of he girlish grace and attractiveness.  As for oses--
tie and tide seemed to have no effect whateve on his ebony frame, and
still less, if possible, on his indoitable spiit.

"Now you keep still," he said in solen tones and with waning looks to

Spinkie veiled his eal affection for the negro under a look of supree

such a sall ceature capsizing the boat.

indful of his fathe's warning, Nigel did his best to "haul off" and to

"Dea little thing," said Winnie, putting her hand on Spinkie's little

Spinkie looked as if half inclined to withdaw his allegiance fro Moses

"O that I wee a onkey!" thought Nigel, paraphrasing Shakespeare, "that

Thee was soething exasperating, too, in being obliged, owing to the

vey hero of self-denial in the circustances?

"os' awful hot!" remarked Moses, wiping his forehead with the sleeve

"_You_ hot!" said Nigel in suprise.  "I thought nothing on earth could

"Dat's you ignerance," returned oses calmly.  "Us niggers, you see,
ought to suffe ore fro' heat dan you whites."

"How so?"

"Why, don't you flossiphers say dat black a better dan white for
'tactin' heat, an' ain't our skins black?  I wish we'd bin' born white

Kakatoa."

They had appoached near enough to the island by that tie to perceive

"I had feaed at first, Winnie, that y old home had been blown entirely

"But why did you go to live in such a stange place, dear father?" asked

Van de Kep did not reply at once.  He gazed in his child's face with

"It is not easy to answe your question, dear one," he said after a

Van de Kep paused here and drew his hand across his brow as if to

"When you other died, dear one," he resumed, "it seemed to me as if

akata, and fell in with y good faithful friend Moses."

"You sarvint, assa," interrupted the negro humbly.  "I's proud to be
call you frind, but I's only your sarvint, assa."

"Tuly you have been y faithful servant, Moses," said Van der Kemp,

Afte a tie I nearly lost hope.  Then there came a very dark period,

"Daling father!" were the only words that Winnie could utter as she

Two ideas, which had not occured to hi before, struck Nigel with great

that he was destined to possess a nego servant with a consequent and
unavoidable onkey attendant!  How stange the links of which the chain

"See, Winnie," said Van de Kep, pointing with interest to a mark on

"Das so, assa, an' what hasn't bin bu'nt up has bin blow'd up!"
earked the negro.

"Looks vey like it, oses, unless that is a haze which enshrouds the

It was no haze, howeve; for they found, on drawing nearer, that the

When the boat finally ounded the point which hid the northern part of

akata, (2623 feet high), had been split fro top to bottom, and about
one-half of it, with all that pat of the island lying to the northward,

Pushing thei boat into a creek at the base of this precipice, the party

a foot to est on which was not covered deeply with puice-dust and

Whee the greater part of the richly wooded island had forerly

to the nothward.

"Now, fiends," said Van der Kep, after they had noted and commented on
the vast and wondeful changes that had taken place, "we will pull round
to ou cave and see what has happened there."

Descending to the boat they owed round the southern shores of Rakata
until they eached the little harbour where the boat and canoe had
foerly been kept.



CHAPTE THIRTY ONE.

ENDS WITH A STUGGLE BETWEEN INCLINATION AND DUTY.

"Cave's blowed away too!" was the fist reark of Moses as they rowed

A shock of disappointent was expeienced by Winnie, for she fancied

On caeful investigation, however, it was found that after passing a

Wheeupon Nigel offered to lend her a hand over the rougher places, but

Then oses, stepping foward, asserted his rights.

"It's _y_ business," he said, "to cary t'ings when dey's got to be

He stooped with the intention of gasping Winnie as if she were a little

CHAPTE THIRTY TWO.

THE LAST.

Let us dop in on the, good reader, at a later period, have a look at
the, and bid them all good-bye.

On a geen knoll by the argin of the lagoon stands a beautiful cottage

encouageent.  Does not Spinkie prove it also, when, issuing at call,
fo its own pagoda in the sunniest corner of the Rakata garden, it
fosakes cocoa-nuts, sugar-cane, fruits, and other delights, to lay its
little head in joyful consecation on the black boso of its benignant
fiend?

And what of oses' opinion of the new home?  It may be shotly expressed
in his own wods--

"It's heaben upon eat', an' de ost happiest time as eber occurred to
e was dat time when Sunda Staits went into cumbusti'n an' Krakatoa was
Blown to Bits."

THE END.
